<span class="italic"><a title="Link to journal home page" href="https://doi.org/10.1039/2040-3372/2009">Nanoscale</a></span>, 2018, <strong>10</strong>, 9218-9225</div><h1 id="sect172"><span class="title_heading">Pseudocapacitance-boosted ultrafast Na storage in a pie-like FeS@C nanohybrid as an advanced anode material for sodium-ion full batteries<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a></span></h1><p class="header_text">
      <span class="bold">

            Cao
          
        
      </span><span class="orcid"><a target="_blank" title="Select to open ORCID record for An-Min&nbsp;Cao (orcid.org/0000-0002-8120-7577) in a new window" id="connect-orcid-link" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0002-8120-7577"><img id="orcid-id-logo" src="/content/NewImages/orcid_16x16.png" alt="ORCID logo"></a></span><sup><span class="sup_ref italic">b</span></sup>,

      <br><a id="affa"><sup><span class="sup_ref italic">a</span></sup></a><span class="italic">National &amp; Local United Engineering Laboratory for Power Batteries, Faculty of Chemistry, Northeast Normal University, Changchun, Jilin 130024, P. R. China. E-mail: <a href="mailto:xinglong@nenu.edu.cn">xinglong@nenu.edu.cn</a></span>
      <br><a id="affb"><sup><span class="sup_ref italic">b</span></sup></a><span class="italic">Beijing National Laboratory for Molecular Sciences (BNLMS), Key Laboratory of Molecular Nanostructure and Nanotechnology, Institute of Chemistry, Chinese Academy of Sciences (CAS), Beijing 100190, PR China</span>
    </p><div id="art-admin"><span class="italic bold">Received 
      28th December 2017
    </span><span class="bold italic">, Accepted 12th April 2018</span></div><p class="bold italic">First published on 12th April 2018</p><hr><div class="abstract"><h2>Abstract</h2><p>In order to develop promising anode materials for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), a novel pie-like FeS@C (P-FeS@C) nanohybrid, in which all ultrasmall FeS nanocrystals (NCs) are completely embedded into the carbon network and sealed by a protective carbon shell, has been prepared. The unique pie-like structure can effectively speed up the kinetics of electrode reactions, while the carbon shell stabilizes the FeS NCs inside. Studies show that the electrochemical reaction processes of P-FeS@C electrodes are dominated by the pseudocapacitive behavior, leading to an ultrafast Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>-insertion/extraction reaction. Hence, the prepared P-FeS@C nanohybrid exhibits superior Na-storage properties especially high rate capability in half cells. For example, it can deliver reversible capacities of 555.1 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at 0.2 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> over 150 cycles and about 60.4 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at 80 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> (an ultrahigh current density even higher than that of the capacitor test). Furthermore, an advanced P-FeS@C//Na<small><sub>3</sub></small>V<small><sub>2</sub></small>(PO<small><sub>4</sub></small>)<small><sub>2</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>F full cell has been assembled out, which delivers a stable specific capacity of 441.2 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> after 80 cycles at 0.5 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> with a capacity retention of 91.8%.</p></div><hr>
    
      <h2 id="sect266"><span class="a_heading">Introduction</span></h2>
      <span>In recent years, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have attracted a great deal of attention as a promising alternative for lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) towards stationary energy storage because of the high natural abundance of Na resources in the Earth's crust and much lower prices.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit1"><sup><span class="sup_ref">1–4</span></sup></a> However, the ionic radius of Na is larger than that of Li, which leads to a much more sluggish Na-diffusion kinetics and makes it difficult to match with some traditional anode materials of LIBs, such as the market-dominant graphite anode.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit5"><sup><span class="sup_ref">5–7</span></sup></a> Therefore, it is highly imperative to develop superior anode materials with high Na-storage capacity, high rate capability and long-term cycle life.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit8"><sup><span class="sup_ref">8–10</span></sup></a> To date, various anode materials have been studied, including alloying elements, metal oxides and transition-metal chalcogenides.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit1"><sup><span class="sup_ref">1,11–13</span></sup></a> Among them, transition-metal chalcogenides based on conversion reactions are attracting more and more attention owing to their high theoretical Na-storage capacity.</span>
      <p class="otherpara">Considering its high abundance in nature, cheap raw materials and environmental benignity, FeS has been proposed as a promising anode material for batteries.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit11"><sup><span class="sup_ref">11</span></sup></a> Previously, FeS has been extensively studied in LIBs for a long time. Nevertheless, less reports are majored on SIBs, which should be mainly due to the huge volume variation during the successive de-/sodiation processes and its low electronic conductivity, leading to the poor cycling stability and rate capability.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit14"><sup><span class="sup_ref">14,15</span></sup></a> To solve these problems, preparing nanometer-sized structures and constructing hybrid electrodes with an appropriate carbon network have been proven to be two effective methods.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit16"><sup><span class="sup_ref">16,17</span></sup></a> While the nanometer-sized materials can relax the strain caused by the volume variation and shorten the diffusion pathway of sodium ions to improve the rate capability, the appropriate carbon networks provide fast electron conducting pathways and serve as a skeleton to improve the structural stability of the whole electrodes.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit18"><sup><span class="sup_ref">18,19</span></sup></a> Thus, it is crucial to design an ideal FeS/C hybrid structure which can combine the advantages of both methods.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit20"><sup><span class="sup_ref">20–22</span></sup></a></p>
      <p class="otherpara">Herein, we have successfully prepared a novel pie-like FeS@C (P-FeS@C) nanohybrid, in which all ultrasmall FeS nanocrystals (NCs) are totally embedded into the carbon network and sealed by a protective carbon shell with the existence of rich mesopores. It was found that the unique pie-like structure can effectively speed up the electrochemical reaction kinetics and stabilize the active FeS NCs, thereby making the prepared FeS@C nanohybrid exhibit a pseudocapacitance-boosted ultrafast sodium storage performance and superior cycling stability when used as an anode material in SIBs. It delivers high reversible Na-storage capacities of 547.1 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at 0.05 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> and 206.2 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at a high current density of 35 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>. More surprisingly, even at an ultrahigh current density of 80 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, a value higher than is mostly used for the capacitor test, the delivered specific capacity is still 60.4 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>. Meanwhile, a high cycling stability was also exhibited, <span class="italic">e.g.</span>, a Na-storage capacity of 555.1 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at 0.2 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> over 150 cycles without any capacity decay. Moreover, an advanced P-FeS@C//NVPOF (here, NVPOF represents a high-voltage cathode of Na<small><sub>3</sub></small>V<small><sub>2</sub></small>(PO<small><sub>4</sub></small>)<small><sub>2</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>F recently developed by us)<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit23"><sup><span class="sup_ref">23</span></sup></a> full cell was further assembled, which delivers a reversible capacity of 441.2 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> after 80 cycles based on the mass of the P-FeS@C anode material at 0.5 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>.</p>
    
    
      <h2 id="sect297"><span class="a_heading">Experimental</span></h2>
      
        <h3 id="sect299"><span class="b_heading">Preparation of P-FeS@C</span></h3>
        <span>Briefly, Tween-80 surfactant (5 mL) and deionized water (150 mL) were mixed together by ultrasonication. Meanwhile, FeCl<small><sub>3</sub></small>·6H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O (2 g) and CO(NH<small><sub>2</sub></small>)<small><sub>2</sub></small> (4.5 g) were dissolved into 10 mL ethanol and 10 mL deionized water, respectively. Next, the FeCl<small><sub>3</sub></small> solution and the CO(NH<small><sub>2</sub></small>)<small><sub>2</sub></small> solution were added into Tween-80 containing solution to form a homogeneous suspension by ultrasonication. After another stirring of 6 hours at 120 °C in an oil bath, the resulting precursors were centrifuged, washed with ethanol (thrice) and dried at 80 °C for 10 h. Finally, the as-prepared precursor was mixed with S powders by grinding and then annealing at 500 °C for 4 h in a quartz tube under an inert N<small><sub>2</sub></small> atmosphere with a heating rate of 2 °C min<small><sup>−1</sup></small>. The mass ratio of the as-prepared precursor and S powders was 1<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">1.</span>
      
      
        <h3 id="sect311"><span class="b_heading">Material characterization</span></h3>
        <span>The morphologies of all products were characterized by scanning electron microscopy (SEM, HITACHI-SU8010, 10 kV) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM, JEOL-2100 F, 200 kV) with an energy dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDX) analysis. X-ray diffraction patterns (XPS) were collected on a Rigaku D/max200PC diffractometer using Cu Kα radiation (<span class="italic">λ</span> = 1.5406 Å). X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy data (XRD) were obtained using an ESCALab220i-XL electron spectrometer from VG Scientific using 300 W Al Kα radiation. The N<small><sub>2</sub></small> adsorption–desorption curve was obtained from an Automatic Micropore and Mesopore Analyzer (ASAP 2020M). The thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) curve was obtained under an air atmosphere with a heating rate of 10 °C min<small><sup>−1</sup></small>.</span>
      
      
        <h3 id="sect317"><span class="b_heading">Electrochemical measurements</span></h3>
        <span>Electrochemical performances were analyzed in the CR2032 coin-type cells. The working electrodes were prepared by mixing the active material, acetylene black and carboxymethylcellulose sodium (CMC) in a weight ratio of 80<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">10<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">10 in deionized water. Then the as-obtained mixture was coated on copper foil followed by vacuum-drying at 60 °C for 12 h before assembly and test. The loading density of the active material on the work electrodes is about 1.5–2.0 mg cm<small><sup>−2</sup></small>. The electrolyte solution was 1.0 mol L<small><sup>−1</sup></small> NaClO<small><sub>4</sub></small> in ethylene carbonate (EC)/propylene carbonate (PC) (1<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">1 by volume), plus 5 wt% FEC. Glass fiber (GF/D) from Whatman were used as a separator and pure sodium foils were used as a counter electrode. The cell assembly was conducted in an Ar-filled glovebox followed by an overnight aging before test. For full-cell assembly, Na<small><sub>3</sub></small>V<small><sub>2</sub></small>(PO<small><sub>4</sub></small>)<small><sub>2</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>F was used instead of the metallic sodium. The Na<small><sub>3</sub></small>V<small><sub>2</sub></small>(PO<small><sub>4</sub></small>)<small><sub>2</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>F electrodes were prepared using the same procedures as the preparation of Fe<small><sub>3</sub></small>O<small><sub>4</sub></small>@FeS anodes. In order to match the cathode/anode capacity, there is a slight excess capacity of anode compared with the cathode, and the mass ratio of P-FeS@C to Na<small><sub>3</sub></small>V<small><sub>2</sub></small>(PO<small><sub>4</sub></small>)<small><sub>2</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>F was about 1<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">4. Prior to the fabrication of full cells, chemical pre-sodiation for the P-FeS@C anode was performed to activate the material and stabilize the electrode surface. Cyclic voltammograms (CVs) were performed on the CHI 606E electrochemical workstation at different scan rates from 0.01 to 2.8 V. The galvanostatic charge/discharge tests were carried out on the LAND-CT2001A battery-testing system in the voltage range of 0.01–2.8 V <span class="italic">vs.</span> Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/Na at various current densities. Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) was performed with an amplitude voltage of 5 mV in the frequency range from 1 MHz to 100 mHz by using a PMC2000 (Princeton Applied Research).</span>
      
    
    
      <h2 id="sect342"><span class="a_heading">Results and discussion</span></h2>
      <span>The P-FeS@C nanohybrid was successfully prepared by a simple solution reaction followed by an annealing process with sulfur (S) powder as shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1a</a>. <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1b</a> is the XRD pattern of the prepared product, showing that all the peaks are well indexed to the standard card of FeS (PDF#65-9124),<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit24"><sup><span class="sup_ref">24</span></sup></a> except for the very broad peak centered at 23° assigned to amorphous carbon in the nanohybrid. Meanwhile, the TGA was carried out to determine the FeS content of around 85 wt% (Fig. S1<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a>) in the P-FeS@C.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit25"><sup><span class="sup_ref">25</span></sup></a> The preparation procedures for the products are further discussed in detail. Firstly, the Fe-containing precursor (a coordination compound of [Fe{OC(NH<small><sub>2</sub></small>)<small><sub>2</sub></small>}<small><sub>6</sub></small>]Cl<small><sub>3</sub></small>) was derived from the solution reaction by <span class="italic">in situ</span> nucleation growth under the induction of the surfactant (Tween-80). <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1c</a> is the SEM image of such an Fe-containing precursor which is composed of urchin-like particles with a diameter of about 500 nm. After annealing with S powder, a pie-like particle was formed under the effect of high temperature and the stress caused by rapid vaporization of sulfur powder, as shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1d</a> and S2.<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a> In this procedure, the S powder plays two roles, <span class="italic">viz</span>., (1) the sulfur sources were used to sulfurize the Fe-containing precursor to form FeS; (2) the raw materials to produce sulfur vapor quickly to promote the formation of P-FeS@C nanohybrids. The SEM image (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1d</a>) of P-FeS@C shows that the diameter of the pie-like particles was about 200 nm and the thickness was about 60 nm. In addition, in the process of sulfurization, the carbon from the precursor has a great effect on the performance of the SIBs, which limits the growth of FeS, resulting in the formation of ultrasmall FeS NCs.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit20"><sup><span class="sup_ref">20,26,27</span></sup></a> The HRTEM image of P-FeS@C (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2a</a>) demonstrates the presence of a lot of ultrasmall FeS NCs (about 4–10 nm), which are fully embedded in the conductive carbon networks and covered by a protective carbon shell with a thickness of 1–2 nm. The ultrasmall FeS NCs and the pie-like structure can shorten the transfer distance of sodium ions, suggesting the enhanced Na-diffusion kinetics.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit28"><sup><span class="sup_ref">28,29</span></sup></a> Furthermore, it can be found that the carbon shell outside has some obvious lattice fringes as shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2a</a>, which indicates that the carbon network has a certain degree of graphitization. In order to further illustrate this point, Raman spectroscopy of P-FeS@C was carried out as shown in Fig. S3.<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a> The Raman spectra present prominent D-bands at about 1330 cm<small><sup>−1</sup></small> (the defect-induced band) and G-bands at about 1560 cm<small><sup>−1</sup></small> (the crystalline graphite band), respectively. The value of <span class="italic">I</span><small><sub>D</sub></small>/<span class="italic">I</span><small><sub>G</sub></small> was 1.1, which means that the carbon networks in P-FeS@C do have a certain degree of graphitization which is highly beneficial for improving the electronic conductivity and structural stability.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit26"><sup><span class="sup_ref">26,30</span></sup></a><a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2b</a> shows the element mappings of C, O, S and Fe in the P-FeS@C, where the uniform distribution of each element demonstrated the homogeneity of the as-prepared materials.</span>
      <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig1"><a href="/image/article/2018/NR/c7nr09674g/c7nr09674g-f1_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: c7nr09674g-f1.tif" src="/image/article/2018/NR/c7nr09674g/c7nr09674g-f1.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 1 </b> <span id="fig1"><span class="graphic_title">(a) The preparation procedures, (b) XRD pattern, and (d) SEM image of the P-FeS@C nanohybrid. (c) SEM image of the precursor for the preparation of P-FeS@C.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig2"><a href="/image/article/2018/NR/c7nr09674g/c7nr09674g-f2_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: c7nr09674g-f2.tif" src="/image/article/2018/NR/c7nr09674g/c7nr09674g-f2.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 2 </b> <span id="fig2"><span class="graphic_title">(a) HRTEM image and (b) the elemental (C, O, S and Fe) mapping images of the P-FeS@C nanohybrid.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      <p class="otherpara">The isothermal nitrogen adsorption/desorption measurement was further carried out to study the pore properties. <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3a</a> shows the typical nitrogen sorption isotherms of P-FeS@C which are type IV adsorption isotherms, indicating the existence of many mesopores in them as reported previously.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit2"><sup><span class="sup_ref">2,31,32</span></sup></a> The fitting results (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3b</a> and S4<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a>) show that the mesopore size in P-FeS@C is concentrated at 3 nm, which should be caused by the sulphur vapour. The rich pore structure is helpful to alleviate the volume change during charging and discharging, and meanwhile they can improve the migration speed of the Na ion.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit10"><sup><span class="sup_ref">10,33,34</span></sup></a> The chemical state and molecular environment of P-FeS@C were characterized by XPS. XPS full spectra confirm the presence of C, O, S and Fe as shown in Fig. S5.<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a> In the C 1s spectrum (shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3c</a>), the peaks present at binding energies of 284.6 eV, 285.6 eV, and 288.5 eV correspond to the C–C, C–O, and C<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_e001.gif" alt="[double bond, length as m-dash]">O functional groups, respectively.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit35"><sup><span class="sup_ref">35–37</span></sup></a> Small amounts of C–O and C<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_e001.gif" alt="[double bond, length as m-dash]">O functional groups are in favor of providing active sites to immobilize FeS. <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3d</a> shows the O 1s XPS spectrum of P-FeS@C, the peaks at 529.9, 531.5 and 532.8 eV correspond to the Fe–O, C–O and O–C<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_e001.gif" alt="[double bond, length as m-dash]">O functional groups, respectively.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit38"><sup><span class="sup_ref">38–40</span></sup></a> The presence of Fe–O proves that there is an interaction between FeS and the carbon shell in the materials. In the S 2p spectrum (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3e</a>), the peak at 161.5 eV is the characteristic peak of FeS corresponding to S<small><sup>2−</sup></small>, whereas the polysulfide (S<small><sub>n</sub></small><small><sup>2−</sup></small>) is detected following oxidation, and the peak at 168.1 eV is the spin–orbit of S 2p1/2.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit41"><sup><span class="sup_ref">41</span></sup></a><a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3f</a> shows the Fe 2p XPS spectrum of P-FeS@C. The peaks observed at the binding energies of 708.7 eV and 722.4 eV can be ascribed to the Fe<small><sup>2+</sup></small> states of FeS. And the peaks at binding energies of 711.2 eV and 724.2 eV can be ascribed to Fe<small><sup>3+</sup></small> states which are characteristic of Fe<small><sub>2</sub></small>O<small><sub>3</sub></small> rather than FeS, this is because the surface of FeS is converted into Fe<small><sub>2</sub></small>O<small><sub>3</sub></small> due to its high reactivity towards oxygen.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit42"><sup><span class="sup_ref">42</span></sup></a></p>
      <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig3"><a href="/image/article/2018/NR/c7nr09674g/c7nr09674g-f3_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: c7nr09674g-f3.tif" src="/image/article/2018/NR/c7nr09674g/c7nr09674g-f3.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 3 </b> <span id="fig3"><span class="graphic_title">(a) N<small><sub>2</sub></small> adsorption/desorption isotherms, (b) the corresponding BJH pore size distribution, and the high-resolution XPS spectra of (c) C 1s, (d) O 1s, (e) S 2p and (f) Fe 2p of the P-FeS@C nanohybrid.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      <p class="otherpara">The electrochemical properties of the P-FeS@C nanohybrid for Na storage were discussed in the half cell in detail. The annealing temperature and time were optimized firstly. Fig. S6<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a> shows the XRD patterns of P-FeS@C with different annealing temperatures (450, 500 and 600 °C). It reveals that FeS can only be formed in more than 500 °C. P-FeS@C at 500 °C shows a better rate capability and cycling stability compared to P-FeS@C at 600 °C (Fig. S7<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a>), owing to the pie-like structure formed at 500 °C rather than the bulk at 600 °C as shown in Fig. S8.<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a> The XRD patterns (Fig. S9<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a>), SEM images (Fig. S10<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a>) and sodium storage performance (Fig. S11<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a>) of P-FeS@C prepared at 500 °C with different times were analysed. The results show that the P-FeS@C can be obtained only by annealing at 500 °C for more than 4 h. When the annealing time is 2 h, although the pie-like structure was formed, Fe<small><sub>2</sub></small>O<small><sub>3</sub></small> was not fully converted into FeS according to the XRD patterns. And when the annealing time was 8 h, the pie-like structure was destroyed. Thus, it is foreseeable that the FeS@C prepared at 500 °C for 4 h shows the best sodium storage performance, and it is the best condition to obtain P-FeS@C.</p>
      <p class="otherpara">
        <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4a</a> presents the cyclic voltammograms (CVs) of P-FeS@C with the best performance for the first 5 cycles in a voltage range of 0.01–2.8 V. The first cathodic scan of P-FeS@C shows a large peak at 0.7 V and a small peak at 0.32 V which are probably related to the formation of solid-electrolyte interphase (SEI) layers and the conversion reactions from FeS to Fe and Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>S.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit26"><sup><span class="sup_ref">26,41</span></sup></a> During the anodic scans, two oxidation peaks are observed at 1.41 and 1.8 V, attributable to the formation of Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>FeS and FeS, respectively. From the second cathodic scan, the peak at 1.77 V is attributed to the insertion reaction of FeS + <span class="italic">x</span>Na + <span class="italic">x</span>e<small><sup>−</sup></small> → Na<small><sub><span class="italic">s</span></sub></small>FeS and the peaks at 0.96 and 0.29 V correspond to the conversion reaction of Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>FeS into Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>S and Fe. It should be noted that the reductive/oxidation peaks at 0.01 and 0.11 are attributed to the insertion/extraction of Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> into the carbon shell of P-FeS@C.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit14"><sup><span class="sup_ref">14,43</span></sup></a> The subsequent four CV curves show good repetition, indicating the good stability of P-FeS@C for Na-ion storage.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit14"><sup><span class="sup_ref">14,44</span></sup></a><a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4b</a> shows the galvanostatic charge/discharge curves of P-FeS@C for the initial 5 cycles at a current density of 0.05 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>. The voltage plateau features are consistent with the CV curves. The discharge and charge capacities of the first cycle are 715.2 and 547.1 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> with a coulombic efficiency of 76.5%. The initial irreversible capacity loss could be attributed to the formation of the SEI layer. The coulombic efficiency of P-FeS@C is at a relatively high level for the same type of materials which is attributed to the fully covered carbon shell of P-FeS@C.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit14"><sup><span class="sup_ref">14,15,26,41,42</span></sup></a> The carbon shell can avoid the direct contact of active materials and electrolytes to reduce the occurrence of side reactions and to form a stable SEI film. The cycling stability of P-FeS@C is shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4c</a>. It exhibits a high reversible capacity of 555.1 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at 0.2 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> over 150 cycles, without any capacity decay. The discharge capacities increased gradually during cycling, primarily due to the formation of a polymeric gel-like film on the surface of P-FeS@C resulting from electrolyte degradation, which has been observed in other transition metal oxides.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit25"><sup><span class="sup_ref">25</span></sup></a><a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5</a> shows the characterization of P-FeS@C after 100 cycles. The pie-like structure can be still observed from the SEM image (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5a</a>), which verifies the stability of P-FeS@C. Furthermore, the HRTEM image indicates that the FeS NCs are still existent as shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5b</a>, although the size of the FeS has increased, suggesting that the carbon network in the pie-like structure can effectively prevent the aggregation of the FeS NCs. In addition, the thickness of the carbon layers outside has also increased compared to the initial one as shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2a</a>, it may be attributed to the growth of the SEI film on the surface of the carbon layers during the charging/discharging process.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit9"><sup><span class="sup_ref">9,35,45</span></sup></a><a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5c</a> shows the element mappings of C, O, S and Fe in P-FeS@C after 100 cycles, where the uniform distribution of every element also proves the structure stability of the pie-like structure.</p>
      <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig4"><a href="/image/article/2018/NR/c7nr09674g/c7nr09674g-f4_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: c7nr09674g-f4.tif" src="/image/article/2018/NR/c7nr09674g/c7nr09674g-f4.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 4 </b> <span id="fig4"><span class="graphic_title">(a) CV curves at 0.1 mV s<small><sup>−1</sup></small> and (b) charge/discharge curves of the initial 5 cycles at 0.05 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> of the P-FeS@C nanohybrid in the half cells. (c) The comparison of cycling stability at 0.2 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> between the P-FeS@C nanohybrid and C-FeS in the half cells.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig5"><a href="/image/article/2018/NR/c7nr09674g/c7nr09674g-f5_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: c7nr09674g-f5.tif" src="/image/article/2018/NR/c7nr09674g/c7nr09674g-f5.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 5 </b> <span id="fig5"><span class="graphic_title">The (a) SEM and (b) HRTEM images, and (c) the elemental (C, O, S and Fe) mappings of the P-FeS@C nanohybrid electrode after 100 cycles.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      <p class="otherpara">Furthermore, the rate capability of P-FeS@C was explored at different densities (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig6">Fig. 6a and b</a>). The reversible capacities of P-FeS@C are 547.1, 479.4, 448.9, 370.2, and 311.1 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at 0.05, 0.5, 2, 7, to 15 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, respectively. Even at a super high current density of 25, 35, 45, 60 and 80 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, the capacities are still 249.7, 206.2, 162.2, 105.7 to 60.4 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>. When the current density decreased to 0.05 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, the reversible capacity was recovered to 578.9 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> after 120 cycles. As a comparison, the sodium storage performance of the commercial FeS (C-FeS) was also studied. Obviously, the C-FeS displays a poor rate capability and cycling stability which is mainly due to the low conductivity and the bulk structure (Fig. S12<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a>). Fig. S13<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a> shows the XRD pattern of C-FeS. The rate capability of the iron sulfide based anode materials for sodium ion storage reported up to now are summarized in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig6">Fig. 6d</a>. P-FeS@C in this work shows excellent rate capability, especially at high rates, as anode materials for sodium ion batteries compared with iron-sulfide-based materials reported in the literatures.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit11"><sup><span class="sup_ref">11,13–15,25,26,41,42,46</span></sup></a> The outstanding performance of P-FeS@C stems from the unique pie-like structure as shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig6">Fig. 6c</a>. First, the ultrasmall FeS NCs and the mesopores in the materials shorten the transfer distance of sodium ions to speed up the Na-ion diffusion kinetics. Second, the thin carbon shell outside produces excellent structure stability and allows helpful electronic contact. Furthermore, the electrochemical sodium storage properties of P-FeS@C are diagnosed using EIS. Fig. S14<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a> shows the Nyquist plots of P-FeS@C and C-FeS after the first cycle in the frequency range of 1 MHz–0.1 Hz, both of which contain a depressed semicircle in the medium-to-high frequencies and a linear tail in the low frequencies. The diameter of the semicircle for P-FeS@C is much smaller than that of C-FeS, suggesting that P-FeS@C has a smaller contact and charge-transfer impedances.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit47"><sup><span class="sup_ref">47</span></sup></a> It also validates that such a novel pie-like structure can effectively improve the electrical conductivity of the FeS. As regards the linear tail in the low frequencies corresponding to the sodium-diffusion process, the apparent Na diffusion coefficient (<span class="italic">D</span><small><sub>apparent</sub></small>) of P-FeS@C and C-FeS was compared using the following equation:<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit48"><sup><span class="sup_ref">48</span></sup></a></p><table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn1"><span id="eqn1"><span class="italic">D</span><small><sub>apparent</sub></small> = <span class="italic">R</span><small><sup>2</sup></small><span class="italic">T</span><small><sup>2</sup></small>/2<span class="italic">A</span><small><sup>2</sup></small><span class="italic">n</span><small><sup>4</sup></small><span class="italic">F</span><small><sup>4</sup></small><span class="italic">C</span><small><sup>2</sup></small><span class="italic">σ</span><small><sup>2</sup></small>,</span></span></td><td class="rightEqn">(1)</td></tr></tbody></table>in which <span class="italic">R</span> and <span class="italic">T</span> are the gas constant and the absolute temperature, <span class="italic">A</span> is the surface area of the electrode, <span class="italic">n</span> is the electron transfer number of per molecule during the charging/discharging process, <span class="italic">F</span> is the Faraday's constant, <span class="italic">C</span> the concentration of sodium ions (1 × 10<small><sup>−3</sup></small> mol cm<small><sup>−3</sup></small>) and <span class="italic">σ</span> is the Warburg factor related to <span class="italic">Z</span><small><sub>re</sub></small>, furthermore, <span class="italic">Z</span><small><sub>re</sub></small> and <span class="italic">σ</span> satisfy the following equation:<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit49"><sup><span class="sup_ref">49</span></sup></a><table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn2"><span id="eqn2"><span class="italic">Z</span><small><sub>re</sub></small> = <span class="italic">R</span><small><sub>D</sub></small> + <span class="italic">R</span><small><sub>L</sub></small> + <span class="italic">σω</span><small><sup>−1/2</sup></small>.</span></span></td><td class="rightEqn">(2)</td></tr></tbody></table><p></p>
      <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig6"><a href="/image/article/2018/NR/c7nr09674g/c7nr09674g-f6_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: c7nr09674g-f6.tif" src="/image/article/2018/NR/c7nr09674g/c7nr09674g-f6.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 6 </b> <span id="fig6"><span class="graphic_title">(a) The comparison of rate capability at different current densities between the P-FeS@C nanohybrid and C-FeS, (b) the charge/discharge curves of the P-FeS@C nanohybrid at various current densities in the half cells. (c) Schematic of electrons and ion transport in the P-FeS@C nanohybrid. (b) The comparison of the rate capability between the present P-FeS@C and other iron-sulphide-based anode materials previously reported in high-quality journals in recent two years.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      <p class="otherpara">Fig. S15<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a> shows the variations and fittings of <span class="italic">Z</span><small><sub>re</sub></small> and <span class="italic">ω</span><small><sup>−1/2</sup></small> (<span class="italic">ω</span> is the angular frequency) in the low-frequency region. It is clear that the slope <span class="italic">σ</span> of C-FeS is about twice as much as P-FeS@C, and the <span class="italic">D</span><small><sub>apparent</sub></small> can be compared from above <a title="" href="#eqn1">eqn (1)</a>. The <span class="italic">D</span><small><sub>apparent</sub></small> of P-FeS@C is much larger than the one of C-FeS, confirming that a faster sodium ion diffusion process is operated in P-FeS@C. It is mainly due to the rich mesopores and ultrasmall FeS NCs in the pie-like structure which greatly shorten the ion transport distance and speed up the reaction kinetics. Hence, the pie-like structure is an ideal structure to achieve high rate capability and great cycling stability.</p>
      <p class="otherpara">The superior rate capability prompted us to study the electrode process kinetics of P-FeS@C. Thus, the CV tests at different scan rates were carried out as shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig7">Fig. 7a</a>. Obviously, the peak current (i) is not proportional to the square root of the scan rate (v), which suggests that the charge/discharge process contains faradaic and non-faradaic behaviors.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit50"><sup><span class="sup_ref">50</span></sup></a> There is a relationship between the peak current and scan rate as follows:<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit51"><sup><span class="sup_ref">51</span></sup></a></p><table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn3"><span id="eqn3"><span class="italic">i</span> = <span class="italic">av</span><small><sup><span class="italic">b</span></sup></small></span></span></td><td class="rightEqn">(3)</td></tr></tbody></table><table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn4"><span id="eqn4">log(<span class="italic">i</span>) = <span class="italic">b</span> × log(<span class="italic">v</span>) + log(<span class="italic">a</span>)</span></span></td><td class="rightEqn">(4)</td></tr></tbody></table>where <span class="italic">a</span> and <span class="italic">b</span> are the adjustable parameters. If <span class="italic">b</span> = 0.5, the electrochemical process is controlled by the Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> insertion process.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit52"><sup><span class="sup_ref">52</span></sup></a> As <span class="italic">b</span> = 1, the process mainly relies on pseudocapacitive control. <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig7">Fig. 7b</a> shows the log(<span class="italic">i</span>) <span class="italic">vs.</span> log(<span class="italic">v</span>) plot of peaks 1–4 in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig7">Fig. 7a</a>, and the <span class="italic">b</span>-values are 0.72, 0.99, 0.99 and 0.89, respectively, suggesting that the electrochemical reaction processes of P-FeS@C electrodes are dominated by the pseudocapacitive behavior.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit11"><sup><span class="sup_ref">11</span></sup></a><a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig7">Fig. 7c</a> summarizes the pseudocapacitive contribution at various scan rates which is calculated using the following equation:<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit11"><sup><span class="sup_ref">11</span></sup></a><table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn5"><span id="eqn5"><span class="italic">i</span> = <span class="italic">k</span><small><sub>1</sub></small><span class="italic">v</span> + <span class="italic">k</span><small><sub>2</sub></small><span class="italic">v</span><small><sup>1/2</sup></small></span></span></td><td class="rightEqn">(5)</td></tr></tbody></table>where <span class="italic">k</span><small><sub>1</sub></small><span class="italic">v</span> and <span class="italic">k</span><small><sub>2</sub></small><span class="italic">v</span><small><sup>0.5</sup></small> represent the pseudocapacitive and the inserted contributions, respectively. The pseudocapacitive contributions are 84%, 87%, 90%, 91% and 94%, respectively. It is further indicated that the electrochemical process of P-FeS@C is mainly controlled by the pseudocapacitive behaviors. This leads to a fast kinetic process and results in an excellent rate capability. The detail pseudocapacitive fraction at 1 mV s<small><sup>−1</sup></small> is presented in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig7">Fig. 7d</a>. The fast kinetic process is mainly attributed to the unique pie-like structure, in which, the ultrasmall FeS NCs and the rich mesopores can effectively shorten the ion transport distance.<p></p>
      <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig7"><a href="/image/article/2018/NR/c7nr09674g/c7nr09674g-f7_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: c7nr09674g-f7.tif" src="/image/article/2018/NR/c7nr09674g/c7nr09674g-f7.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 7 </b> <span id="fig7"><span class="graphic_title">(a) CV curves at different scan rates, (b) log(<span class="italic">i</span>) <span class="italic">versus</span> log(<span class="italic">v</span>) plot, (c) the pseudocapacitive contribution at different scan rates and (d) CV curve with the pseudocapacitive fraction shown by the green area at a scan rate of 1 mV s<small><sup>−1</sup></small> of the P-FeS@C nanohybrid.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      <p class="otherpara">The excellent electrochemical performance of P-FeS@C encourages us to try to assemble a Na-ion full cell. NVPOF is used as a cathode material which is prepared following the method previously reported by our group.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit23"><sup><span class="sup_ref">23,53,54</span></sup></a> The characteristics of NVPOF are shown in Fig. S16 and S17.<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a> In detail, the P-FeS@C//NVPOF full cell was assembled according to the scheme inserted in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig8">Fig. 8a</a>. The charge and discharge curves of NVPOF and P-FeS@C in the Na-ion half-cell are shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig8">Fig. 8a</a>. <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig8">Fig. 8b</a> shows the charge/discharge curves of the P-FeS@C//NVPOF full cell at 0.1 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> which presents the comprehensive characteristics of both the cathode and anode with an average output voltage about 2.6 V. It is enough to light up 84 LED bulbs containing 48 yellow bulbs and 36 green bulbs as shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig8">Fig. 8c</a>. The P-FeS@C//NVPOF full cell shows an outstanding sodium ion storage performance. As shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig8">Fig. 8d</a>, when the current densities reach 0.05, 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, 1.5, 2 and 5 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, the reversible capacities are 477.2, 454.9, 436.1, 413.1, 386.5, 372.3, 360 and 305.7 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>. <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig8">Fig. 8e</a> shows the cycling stability of the P-FeS@C//NVPOF full cell, delivering a reversible capacity of 441.2 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> after 80 cycles at 0.5 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> with a capacity retention of 91.8% and a coulombic efficiency of about 99.5%. It should be noted that all the capacities are calculated by the mass of P-FeS@C.</p>
      <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig8"><a href="/image/article/2018/NR/c7nr09674g/c7nr09674g-f8_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: c7nr09674g-f8.tif" src="/image/article/2018/NR/c7nr09674g/c7nr09674g-f8.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 8 </b> <span id="fig8"><span class="graphic_title">(a) Charge/discharge curves of NVPOF and the P-FeS@C nanohybrid in the Na-ion half cells at 0.05 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, the inset in (a) is the assembly illustration of the P-FeS@C//NVPOF full cell. (b) Charge/discharge curves of the P-FeS@C//NVPOF full cell at 0.1 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>. (c) A photograph shows that the full battery can light up a LED panel with 48 yellow bulbs and 36 green bulbs. (d) The rate capability and (e) the cycling stability of the P-FeS@C//NVPOF full cells.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
    
    
      <h2 id="sect607"><span class="a_heading">Conclusions</span></h2>
      <span>In summary, a novel pie-like FeS@C (P-FeS@C) nanohybrid, in which all ultrasmall FeS NCs are totally embedded into the carbon network and sealed by a protective carbon shell, has been prepared. The unique pie-like structure has speeded up the reaction kinetics and stabilized the FeS NCs inside. The P-FeS@C nanohybrid exhibits an excellent rate capability and a superior cycling stability as an anode material of SIBs. It delivers 555.1 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at 0.2 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> over 150 cycles, without any capacity decay. Even at 35 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, the reversible capacity is still 140.8 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>. Incredibly, even at a high current density of 80 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, the capacity is still 60.4 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>. Moreover, the P-FeS@C//NVPOF full cell shows a superior sodium ion storage performance, delivering a reversible capacity of 477.2 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at 0.05 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>. It should be noted that even up to 5 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, the reversible capacity was still 305.7 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>. The superior performance of P-FeS@C enables it to become a promising anode material for SIBs. In view of the excellent sodium performance of this pie-like structure, it could also be extended to other cathode and anode materials, which will promote the development of next-generation electrochemical energy storage devices (SIBs/LIBs, supercapacitors and the hybrids) with both high-power and high-energy densities.</span>
